<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Light-emitting diode</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Br20_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A bulb-shaped modern retrofit <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">LED lamp</a> with aluminium <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a>, a light <a href="/wiki/Diffuser_(optics)" title="Diffuser (optics)">diffusing</a> dome and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E27_screw" title="E27 screw">E27 screw</a> base, using a built-in power supply working on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mains_voltage" title="Mains voltage">mains voltage</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <b>light-emitting diode</b> (<b>LED</b>) is a two-<a href="/wiki/Lead_(electronics)" title="Lead (electronics)">lead</a> <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_source" title="Light source">light source</a>. It is a <a href="/wiki/P%E2%80%93n_junction" title="P–n junction">p–n junction</a> <a href="/wiki/Diode" title="Diode">diode</a>, which emits light when activated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> When a suitable <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a> is applied to the leads, <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> are able to recombine with <a href="/wiki/Electron_hole" title="Electron hole">electron holes</a> within the device, releasing energy in the form of <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>. This effect is called <a href="/wiki/Electroluminescence" title="Electroluminescence">electroluminescence</a>, and the color of the light (corresponding to the energy of the photon) is determined by the energy <a href="/wiki/Band_gap" title="Band gap">band gap</a> of the semiconductor.</p>
<p>An LED is often small in area (less than 1 mm<sup>2</sup>) and integrated optical components may be used to shape its <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Appearing as practical electronic components in 1962,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FirstPracticalLED_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FirstPracticalLED-6">[6]</a></sup> the earliest LEDs emitted low-intensity infrared light. Infrared LEDs are still frequently used as transmitting elements in remote-control circuits, such as those in remote controls for a wide variety of consumer electronics. The first visible-light LEDs were also of low intensity and limited to red. Modern LEDs are available across the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> wavelengths, with very high brightness.</p>
<p>Early LEDs were often used as indicator lamps for electronic devices, replacing small incandescent bulbs. They were soon packaged into numeric readouts in the form of <a href="/wiki/Seven-segment_display" title="Seven-segment display">seven-segment displays</a> and were commonly seen in digital clocks.</p>
<p>Recent developments in LEDs permit them to be used in environmental and task lighting. LEDs have many advantages over incandescent light sources including lower energy consumption, longer lifetime, improved physical robustness, smaller size, and faster switching. Light-emitting diodes are now used in applications as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Navigation_light#Aviation_navigation_lights" title="Navigation light">aviation lighting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Automotive_lighting#Light_emitting_diodes_.28LED.29" title="Automotive lighting">automotive headlamps</a>, advertising, <a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">general lighting</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traffic_signal" title="Traffic signal">traffic signals</a>, camera flashes, and lighted wallpaper. As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, LEDs powerful enough for room lighting remain somewhat more expensive, and require more precise current and heat management, than compact <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamp</a> sources of comparable output. They are, however, significantly more energy efficient and, arguably, have fewer environmental concerns linked to their disposal. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfl-epa_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-cfl-epa-7">[7]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>LEDs have allowed new displays and sensors to be developed, while their high switching rates are also used in advanced communications technology.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discoveries_and_early_devices">Discoveries and early devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discoveries and early devices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SiC_LED_historic.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="640" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/SiC_LED_historic.jpg/220px-SiC_LED_historic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/SiC_LED_historic.jpg/330px-SiC_LED_historic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/SiC_LED_historic.jpg/440px-SiC_LED_historic.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SiC_LED_historic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Green electroluminescence from a point contact on a crystal of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">SiC</a> recreates <a href="/wiki/H._J._Round" title="H. J. Round">Round</a>'s original experiment from 1907.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Electroluminescence" title="Electroluminescence">Electroluminescence</a> as a phenomenon was discovered in 1907 by the British experimenter <a href="/wiki/H._J._Round" title="H. J. Round">H. J. Round</a> of <a href="/wiki/Marconi_Company" title="Marconi Company">Marconi Labs</a>, using a crystal of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Cat%27s-whisker_detector" title="Cat's-whisker detector">cat's-whisker detector</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Russian inventor <a href="/wiki/Oleg_Losev" title="Oleg Losev">Oleg Losev</a> reported creation of the first LED in 1927.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Losev1927_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Losev1927-11">[11]</a></sup> His research was distributed in Soviet, German and British scientific journals, but no practical use was made of the discovery for several decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zheludev_100yearhistory_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zheludev_100yearhistory-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Lehovec" title="Kurt Lehovec">Kurt Lehovec</a>, Carl Accardo, and Edward Jamgochian explained these first light-emitting diodes in 1951 using an apparatus employing <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">SiC</a> crystals with a current source of battery or pulse generator and with a comparison to a variant, pure, crystal in 1953.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rubin Braunstein<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_Corporation_of_America" title="Radio Corporation of America">Radio Corporation of America</a> reported on infrared emission from <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">gallium arsenide</a> (GaAs) and other semiconductor alloys in 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Braunstein observed infrared emission generated by simple diode structures using <a href="/wiki/Gallium_antimonide" title="Gallium antimonide">gallium antimonide</a> (GaSb), GaAs, <a href="/wiki/Indium_phosphide" title="Indium phosphide">indium phosphide</a> (InP), and <a href="/wiki/Silicon-germanium" title="Silicon-germanium">silicon-germanium</a> (SiGe) alloys at room temperature and at 77 Kelvin.</p>
<p>In 1957, Braunstein further demonstrated that the rudimentary devices could be used for non-radio communication across a short distance. As noted by Kroemer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Braunstein "…had set up a simple optical communications link: Music emerging from a record player was used via suitable electronics to modulate the forward current of a GaAs diode. The emitted light was detected by a PbS diode some distance away. This signal was fed into an audio amplifier and played back by a loudspeaker. Intercepting the beam stopped the music. We had a great deal of fun playing with this setup." This setup presaged the use of LEDs for optical communication applications.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:213px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SNX100.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="286" data-file-width="211" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/34/SNX100.jpg" width="211"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SNX100.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Texas Instruments SNX-100 GaAs LED contained in a TO-18 transistor metal case.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In September 1961, while working at <a href="/wiki/Texas_Instruments" title="Texas Instruments">Texas Instruments</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_R._Biard" title="James R. Biard">James R. Biard</a> and Gary Pittman discovered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-infrared" title="Near-infrared">near-infrared</a> (900 nm) light emission from a <a href="/wiki/Tunnel_diode" title="Tunnel diode">tunnel diode</a> they had constructed on a GaAs substrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FirstPracticalLED_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FirstPracticalLED-6">[6]</a></sup> By October 1961, they had demonstrated efficient light emission and signal coupling between a GaAs p-n junction light emitter and an electrically-isolated semiconductor photodetector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> On August 8, 1962, Biard and Pittman filed a patent titled "Semiconductor Radiant Diode" based on their findings, which described a zinc diffused <a href="/wiki/P%E2%80%93n_junction" title="P–n junction">p–n junction</a> LED with a spaced <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathode</a> contact to allow for efficient emission of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_light" title="Infrared light">infrared light</a> under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forward_bias" title="Forward bias">forward bias</a>. After establishing the priority of their work based on engineering notebooks predating submissions from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G.E." title="G.E.">G.E.</a> Labs, <a href="/wiki/RCA" title="RCA">RCA</a> Research Labs, <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> Research Labs, <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Labs</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincoln_Lab" title="Lincoln Lab">Lincoln Lab</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="United States Patent and Trademark Office">U.S. patent office</a> issued the two inventors the patent for the GaAs infrared (IR) light-emitting diode (U.S. Patent <a class="external text" href="http://www.freepatentsonline.com/3293513.pdf" rel="nofollow">US3293513</a>), the first practical LED.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FirstPracticalLED_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-FirstPracticalLED-6">[6]</a></sup> Immediately after filing the patent, <a href="/wiki/Texas_Instruments" title="Texas Instruments">Texas Instruments</a> (TI) began a project to manufacture infrared diodes. In October 1962, TI announced the first commercial LED product (the SNX-100), which employed a pure GaAs crystal to emit a 890 nm light output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FirstPracticalLED_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-FirstPracticalLED-6">[6]</a></sup> In October 1963, TI announced the first commercial hemispherical LED, the SNX-110.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first visible-spectrum (red) LED was developed in 1962 by <a href="/wiki/Nick_Holonyak" title="Nick Holonyak">Nick Holonyak, Jr.</a> while working at <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a>. Holonyak first reported his LED in the journal <i>Applied Physics Letters</i> on December 1, 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chisuntimes_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-chisuntimes-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/M._George_Craford" title="M. George Craford">M. George Craford</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> a former graduate student of Holonyak, invented the first yellow LED and improved the brightness of red and red-orange LEDs by a factor of ten in 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In 1976, T. P. Pearsall created the first high-brightness, high-efficiency LEDs for optical fiber telecommunications by inventing new semiconductor materials specifically adapted to optical fiber transmission wavelengths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_commercial_development">Initial commercial development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Initial commercial development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first commercial LEDs were commonly used as replacements for <a href="/wiki/Incandescence" title="Incandescence">incandescent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neon_lamp" title="Neon lamp">neon</a> indicator lamps, and in <a href="/wiki/Seven-segment_display" title="Seven-segment display">seven-segment displays</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> first in expensive equipment such as laboratory and electronics test equipment, then later in such appliances as TVs, radios, telephones, calculators, as well as watches (see list of <a href="#Indicators_and_signs">signal uses</a>). Until 1968, visible and infrared LEDs were extremely costly, in the order of <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>200 per unit, and so had little practical use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schubert_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schubert-27">[27]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monsanto_Company" title="Monsanto Company">Monsanto Company</a> was the first organization to mass-produce visible LEDs, using gallium arsenide phosphide (GaAsP) in 1968 to produce red LEDs suitable for indicators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schubert_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schubert-27">[27]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hewlett_Packard" title="Hewlett Packard">Hewlett Packard</a> (HP) introduced LEDs in 1968, initially using GaAsP supplied by Monsanto. These red LEDs were bright enough only for use as indicators, as the light output was not enough to illuminate an area. Readouts in calculators were so small that plastic lenses were built over each digit to make them legible. Later, other colors became widely available and appeared in appliances and equipment. In the 1970s commercially successful LED devices at less than five cents each were produced by Fairchild Optoelectronics. These devices employed compound semiconductor chips fabricated with the <a href="/wiki/Planar_process" title="Planar process">planar process</a> invented by Dr. Jean Hoerni at <a href="/wiki/Fairchild_Semiconductor" title="Fairchild Semiconductor">Fairchild Semiconductor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The combination of planar processing for chip fabrication and innovative packaging methods enabled the team at Fairchild led by optoelectronics pioneer Thomas Brandt to achieve the needed cost reductions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> These methods continue to be used by LED producers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TI-30-LED-Display-3682e1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="1024" height="55" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/TI-30-LED-Display-3682e1.jpg/220px-TI-30-LED-Display-3682e1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/TI-30-LED-Display-3682e1.jpg/330px-TI-30-LED-Display-3682e1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/TI-30-LED-Display-3682e1.jpg/440px-TI-30-LED-Display-3682e1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TI-30-LED-Display-3682e1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
LED display of a <a href="/wiki/TI-30" title="TI-30">TI-30</a> scientific calculator (ca. 1978), which uses plastic lenses to increase the visible digit size</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most LEDs were made in the very common 5 mm T1¾ and 3 mm T1 packages, but with rising power output, it has grown increasingly necessary to shed excess heat to maintain reliability,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> so more complex packages have been adapted for efficient heat dissipation. Packages for state-of-the-art <a href="#High-power">high-power LEDs</a> bear little resemblance to early LEDs.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_LED">Blue LED</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Blue LED">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Blue LEDs were first developed by Herbert Paul Maruska at RCA in 1972 using gallium nitride (GaN) on a sapphire substrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> SiC-types were first commercially sold in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> by Cree in 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> However, neither of these initial blue LEDs were very bright.</p>
<p>The first high-brightness blue LED was demonstrated by <a href="/wiki/Shuji_Nakamura" title="Shuji Nakamura">Shuji Nakamura</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nichia_Corporation" title="Nichia Corporation">Nichia Corporation</a> in 1994 and was based on <a href="/wiki/Indium_gallium_nitride" title="Indium gallium nitride">InGaN</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nakamura_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nakamura-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In parallel, <a href="/wiki/Isamu_Akasaki" title="Isamu Akasaki">Isamu Akasaki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hiroshi_Amano" title="Hiroshi Amano">Hiroshi Amano</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nagoya" title="Nagoya">Nagoya</a> were working on developing the important <a href="/wiki/Gallium_nitride" title="Gallium nitride">GaN</a> nucleation on sapphire substrates and the demonstration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-type_semiconductor" title="P-type semiconductor">p-type doping</a> of GaN. Nakamura, Akasaki, and Amano were awarded the 2014 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel prize in physics</a> for their work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Barbieri" title="Alberto Barbieri">Alberto Barbieri</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Cardiff_University" title="Cardiff University">Cardiff University</a> Laboratory (GB) investigated the efficiency and reliability of high-brightness LEDs and demonstrated a "transparent contact" LED using <a href="/wiki/Indium_tin_oxide" title="Indium tin oxide">indium tin oxide</a> (ITO) on (AlGaInP/GaAs).</p>
<p>In 2001<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and 2002,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> processes for growing <a href="/wiki/Gallium_nitride" title="Gallium nitride">gallium nitride</a> (GaN) LEDs on <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> were successfully demonstrated. In January 2012, <a href="/wiki/Osram" title="Osram">Osram</a> demonstrated high-power InGaN LEDs grown on silicon substrates commercially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="White_LEDs_and_the_Illumination_breakthrough">White LEDs and the Illumination breakthrough</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: White LEDs and the Illumination breakthrough">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The attainment of high efficiency in blue LEDs was quickly followed by the development of the first <a href="#Phosphor-based_LEDs">white LED</a>. In this device a <span class="chemf nowrap">Y<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
3</span>Al<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
5</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
12</span></span>:Ce (known as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YAG" title="YAG">YAG</a>") phosphor coating on the emitter absorbs some of the blue emission and produces yellow light through <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescence</a>. The combination of that yellow with remaining blue light appears white to the eye. However, using different <a href="/wiki/Phosphor" title="Phosphor">phosphors</a> (fluorescent materials) it also became possible to instead produce green and red light through fluorescence. The resulting mixture of red, green and blue is not only perceived by humans as white light but is superior for illumination in terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color_rendering" title="Color rendering">color rendering</a>, whereas one cannot appreciate the color of red or green objects illuminated only by the yellow (and remaining blue) wavelengths from the YAG phosphor.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haitz_law.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="600" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Haitz_law.svg/320px-Haitz_law.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Haitz_law.svg/480px-Haitz_law.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Haitz_law.svg/640px-Haitz_law.svg.png 2x" width="320"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haitz_law.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration of <a href="/wiki/Haitz%27s_law" title="Haitz's law">Haitz's law</a>, showing improvement in light output per LED over time, with a logarithmic scale on the vertical axis</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first white LEDs were expensive and inefficient. However, the light output of LEDs has increased <a href="/wiki/Exponential_growth" title="Exponential growth">exponentially</a>, with a doubling occurring approximately every 36 months since the 1960s (similar to <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">Moore's law</a>). This trend is generally attributed to the parallel development of other semiconductor technologies and advances in optics<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and <a href="/wiki/Materials_science" title="Materials science">materials science</a> and has been called <a href="/wiki/Haitz%27s_law" title="Haitz's law">Haitz's law</a> after Dr. Roland Haitz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The light output and efficiency of blue and near-ultraviolet LEDs rose as the cost of reliable devices fell: this led to the use of (relatively) high-power white-light LEDs for the purpose of illumination which are replacing incandescent and fluorescent lighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Experimental white LEDs have been demonstrated to produce over 300 lumens per watt of electricity; some can last up to 100,000 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Compared to incandescent bulbs, this is not only a huge increase in electrical efficiency but – over time – a similar or lower cost per bulb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Working_principle">Working principle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Working principle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PnJunction-LED-E.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="278" data-file-width="395" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/PnJunction-LED-E.svg/300px-PnJunction-LED-E.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/PnJunction-LED-E.svg/450px-PnJunction-LED-E.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/PnJunction-LED-E.svg/600px-PnJunction-LED-E.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PnJunction-LED-E.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The inner workings of an LED, showing circuit (top) and <a href="/wiki/Band_diagram" title="Band diagram">band diagram</a> (bottom)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A P-N junction can convert absorbed light energy into a proportional electric current. The same process is reversed here (i.e. the P-N junction emits light when electrical energy is applied to it). This phenomenon is generally called <a href="/wiki/Electroluminescence" title="Electroluminescence">electroluminescence</a>, which can be defined as the emission of light from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-conductor" title="Semi-conductor">semi-conductor</a> under the influence of an <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a>. The charge carriers recombine in a forward-biased P-N junction as the electrons cross from the N-region and recombine with the holes existing in the P-region. Free electrons are in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conduction_band" title="Conduction band">conduction band</a> of energy levels, while holes are in the valence <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_band" title="Energy band">energy band</a>. Thus the energy level of the holes will be lesser than the energy levels of the electrons. Some portion of the energy must be dissipated in order to recombine the electrons and the holes. This energy is emitted in the form of heat and light.</p>
<p>The electrons dissipate energy in the form of heat for silicon and germanium diodes but in <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide_phosphide" title="Gallium arsenide phosphide">gallium arsenide phosphide</a> (GaAsP) and <a href="/wiki/Gallium_phosphide" title="Gallium phosphide">gallium phosphide</a> (GaP) semiconductors, the electrons dissipate energy by emitting <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>. If the semiconductor is translucent, the junction becomes the source of light as it is emitted, thus becoming a light-emitting diode, but when the junction is reverse biased no light will be produced by the LED and, on the contrary, the device may also be damaged.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology">Technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diode-IV-Curve.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="500" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Diode-IV-Curve.svg/300px-Diode-IV-Curve.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Diode-IV-Curve.svg/450px-Diode-IV-Curve.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Diode-IV-Curve.svg/600px-Diode-IV-Curve.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diode-IV-Curve.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
I-V diagram for a <a href="/wiki/Diode" title="Diode">diode</a>. An LED will begin to emit light when more than 2 or 3 volts is applied to it. The reverse bias region uses a different vertical scale from the forward bias region, in order to show that the leakage current is nearly constant with voltage until breakdown occurs. In forward bias, the current is small but increases exponentially with voltage.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The LED consists of a chip of semiconducting material <a href="/wiki/Doping_(semiconductor)" title="Doping (semiconductor)">doped</a> with impurities to create a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-n_junction" title="P-n junction">p-n junction</a></i>. As in other diodes, current flows easily from the p-side, or <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a>, to the n-side, or cathode, but not in the reverse direction. Charge-carriers—<a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron_hole" title="Electron hole">holes</a>—flow into the junction from <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrodes</a> with different voltages. When an electron meets a hole, it falls into a lower <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">energy level</a> and releases <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of the light emitted, and thus its color, depends on the <a href="/wiki/Band_gap" title="Band gap">band gap</a> energy of the materials forming the <i>p-n junction</i>. In <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> diodes, the electrons and holes usually recombine by a <i>non-radiative transition</i>, which produces no optical emission, because these are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indirect_band_gap" title="Indirect band gap">indirect band gap</a> materials. The materials used for the LED have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_band_gap" title="Direct band gap">direct band gap</a> with energies corresponding to near-infrared, visible, or near-ultraviolet light.</p>
<p>LED development began with infrared and red devices made with <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">gallium arsenide</a>. Advances in <a href="/wiki/Materials_science" title="Materials science">materials science</a> have enabled making devices with ever-shorter wavelengths, emitting light in a variety of colors.</p>
<p>LEDs are usually built on an n-type substrate, with an electrode attached to the p-type layer deposited on its surface. P-type substrates, while less common, occur as well. Many commercial LEDs, especially GaN/InGaN, also use <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">sapphire</a> substrate.</p>
<p>Most materials used for LED production have very high <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive indices</a>. This means that much of the light will be reflected back into the material at the material/air surface interface. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Light_extraction_in_LEDs" title="Light extraction in LEDs">light extraction in LEDs</a> is an important aspect of LED production, subject to much research and development.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refractive_index">Refractive index</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Refractive index">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LED-chip-20-deg-crti-angle_-_both_types_-_crop.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="444" data-file-width="991" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/LED-chip-20-deg-crti-angle_-_both_types_-_crop.png/300px-LED-chip-20-deg-crti-angle_-_both_types_-_crop.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/LED-chip-20-deg-crti-angle_-_both_types_-_crop.png/450px-LED-chip-20-deg-crti-angle_-_both_types_-_crop.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/LED-chip-20-deg-crti-angle_-_both_types_-_crop.png/600px-LED-chip-20-deg-crti-angle_-_both_types_-_crop.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LED-chip-20-deg-crti-angle_-_both_types_-_crop.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Idealized example of light emission cones in a semiconductor, for a single point-source emission zone. The left illustration is for a fully translucent wafer, while the right illustration shows the half-cones formed when the bottom layer is fully opaque. The light is actually emitted equally in all directions from the point-source, so the areas between the cones show the large amount of trapped light energy that is wasted as heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-critical_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-critical-47">[47]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LED_light_emission_cones_from_2D_plane_emission_zone.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1840" data-file-width="2048" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/LED_light_emission_cones_from_2D_plane_emission_zone.png/300px-LED_light_emission_cones_from_2D_plane_emission_zone.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/LED_light_emission_cones_from_2D_plane_emission_zone.png/450px-LED_light_emission_cones_from_2D_plane_emission_zone.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/LED_light_emission_cones_from_2D_plane_emission_zone.png/600px-LED_light_emission_cones_from_2D_plane_emission_zone.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LED_light_emission_cones_from_2D_plane_emission_zone.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The light emission cones of a real LED wafer are far more complex than a single point-source light emission. The light emission zone is typically a two-dimensional plane between the wafers. Every atom across this plane has an individual set of emission cones. Drawing the billions of overlapping cones is impossible, so this is a simplified diagram showing the extents of all the emission cones combined. The larger side cones are clipped to show the interior features and reduce image complexity; they would extend to the opposite edges of the two-dimensional emission plane.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bare uncoated semiconductors such as <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> exhibit a very high <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> relative to open air, which prevents passage of photons arriving at sharp angles relative to the air-contacting surface of the semiconductor due to <a href="/wiki/Total_internal_reflection" title="Total internal reflection">total internal reflection</a>. This property affects both the light-emission efficiency of LEDs as well as the light-absorption efficiency of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_cell" title="Photovoltaic cell">photovoltaic cells</a>. The refractive index of silicon is 3.96 (at 590 nm),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-si_website_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-si_website-48">[48]</a></sup> while air is 1.0002926.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>In general, a flat-surface uncoated LED semiconductor chip will emit light only perpendicular to the semiconductor's surface, and a few degrees to the side, in a cone shape referred to as the <i>light cone</i>, <i>cone of light</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> or the <i>escape cone</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-critical_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-critical-47">[47]</a></sup> The maximum <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_incidence_(optics)" title="Angle of incidence (optics)">angle of incidence</a> is referred to as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_angle_(optics)" title="Critical angle (optics)">critical angle</a>. When this angle is exceeded, photons no longer escape the semiconductor but are instead reflected internally inside the semiconductor crystal as if it were a <a href="/wiki/Mirror" title="Mirror">mirror</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-critical_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-critical-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_reflection" title="Internal reflection">Internal reflections</a> can escape through other crystalline faces if the incidence angle is low enough and the crystal is sufficiently transparent to not re-absorb the photon emission. But for a simple square LED with 90-degree angled surfaces on all sides, the faces all act as equal angle mirrors. In this case, most of the light can not escape and is lost as waste heat in the crystal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-critical_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-critical-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>A convoluted chip surface with angled <a href="/wiki/Facet" title="Facet">facets</a> similar to a jewel or <a href="/wiki/Fresnel_lens" title="Fresnel lens">fresnel lens</a> can increase light output by allowing light to be emitted perpendicular to the chip surface while far to the sides of the photon emission point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ideal shape of a semiconductor with maximum light output would be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microsphere" title="Microsphere">microsphere</a> with the photon emission occurring at the exact center, with electrodes penetrating to the center to contact at the emission point. All light rays emanating from the center would be perpendicular to the entire surface of the sphere, resulting in no internal reflections. A hemispherical semiconductor would also work, with the flat back-surface serving as a mirror to back-scattered photons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_coatings">Transition coatings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Transition coatings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After the doping of the <a href="/wiki/Wafer_(electronics)" title="Wafer (electronics)">wafer</a>, it is cut apart into individual <a href="/wiki/Die_(integrated_circuit)" title="Die (integrated circuit)">dies</a>. Each die is commonly called a chip.</p>
<p>Many LED semiconductor chips are encapsulated or <a href="/wiki/Potting_(electronics)" title="Potting (electronics)">potted</a> in clear or colored molded plastic shells. The plastic shell has three purposes:</p>
<ol>
<li>Mounting the semiconductor chip in devices is easier to accomplish.</li>
<li>The tiny fragile electrical wiring is physically supported and protected from damage.</li>
<li>The plastic acts as a refractive intermediary between the relatively high-index semiconductor and low-index open air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>The third feature helps to boost the light emission from the semiconductor by acting as a diffusing lens, allowing light to be emitted at a much higher angle of incidence from the light cone than the bare chip is able to emit alone.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency_and_operational_parameters">Efficiency and operational parameters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Efficiency and operational parameters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Typical indicator LEDs are designed to operate with no more than 30–60 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milliwatt" title="Milliwatt">milliwatts</a> (mW) of electrical power. Around 1999, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philips_Lumileds_Lighting_Company" title="Philips Lumileds Lighting Company">Philips Lumileds</a> introduced power LEDs capable of continuous use at one <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watt</a>. These LEDs used much larger semiconductor die sizes to handle the large power inputs. Also, the semiconductor dies were mounted onto metal slugs to allow for heat removal from the LED die.</p>
<p>One of the key advantages of LED-based lighting sources is high <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">luminous efficacy</a>. White LEDs quickly matched and overtook the efficacy of standard incandescent lighting systems. In 2002, Lumileds made five-watt LEDs available with luminous efficacy of 18–22 lumens per watt (lm/W). For comparison, a conventional <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">incandescent light bulb</a> of 60–100 watts emits around 15 lm/W, and standard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorescent_light" title="Fluorescent light">fluorescent lights</a> emit up to 100 lm/W.</p>
<p>As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">Philips</a> had achieved the following efficacies for each color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The efficiency values show the physics – light power out per electrical power in. The lumen-per-watt efficacy value includes characteristics of the human eye and is derived using the <a href="/wiki/Luminosity_function" title="Luminosity function">luminosity function</a>.</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">

</tr>

<td>37</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In September 2003, a new type of blue LED was demonstrated by <a href="/wiki/Cree_Inc." title="Cree Inc.">Cree</a> that consumes 24 mW at 20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milliampere" title="Milliampere">milliamperes</a> (mA). This produced a commercially packaged white light giving 65 lm/W at 20 mA, becoming the brightest white LED commercially available at the time, and more than four times as efficient as standard incandescents. In 2006, they demonstrated a prototype with a record white LED luminous efficacy of 131 lm/W at 20 mA. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nichia_Corporation" title="Nichia Corporation">Nichia Corporation</a> has developed a white LED with luminous efficacy of 150 lm/W at a forward current of 20 mA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Cree's XLamp XM-L LEDs, commercially available in 2011, produce 100 lm/W at their full power of 10 W, and up to 160 lm/W at around 2 W input power. In 2012, Cree announced a white LED giving 254 lm/W,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and 303 lm/W in March 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Practical general lighting needs high-power LEDs, of one watt or more. Typical operating currents for such devices begin at 350 mA.</p>
<p>These efficiencies are for the light-emitting diode only, held at low temperature in a lab. Since LEDs installed in real fixtures operate at higher temperature and with driver losses, real-world efficiencies are much lower. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a> (DOE) testing of commercial LED lamps designed to replace incandescent lamps or <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">CFLs</a> showed that average efficacy was still about 46 lm/W in 2009 (tested performance ranged from 17 lm/W to 79 lm/W).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doe_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-doe-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency_droop">Efficiency droop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Efficiency droop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><span id="LED_droop"></span><span id="Droop_.28LED.29"></span>Efficiency droop is the decrease in luminous efficiency of LEDs as the <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a> increases above tens of milliamperes.</p>
<p>This effect was initially theorized to be related to elevated temperatures. Scientists proved the opposite to be true: although the life of an LED would be shortened, the efficiency droop is less severe at elevated temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The mechanism causing efficiency droop was identified in 2007 as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auger_recombination" title="Auger recombination">Auger recombination</a>, which was taken with mixed reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stevenson_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-stevenson-60">[60]</a></sup> In 2013, a study confirmed Auger recombination as the cause of efficiency droop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to being less efficient, operating LEDs at higher electric currents creates higher heat levels which compromise the lifetime of the LED. Because of this increased heating at higher currents, <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode#High-power" title="Light-emitting diode">high-brightness LEDs</a> have an industry standard of operating at only 350 mA, which is a compromise between light output, efficiency, and longevity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stevenson_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-stevenson-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_LED.27s_dark_secret_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_LED.27s_dark_secret-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedaily1_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedaily1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_solutions">Possible solutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Possible solutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Instead of increasing current levels, luminance is usually increased by combining multiple LEDs in one bulb. Solving the problem of efficiency droop would mean that household LED light bulbs would need fewer LEDs, which would significantly reduce costs.</p>
<p>Researchers at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Naval_Research_Laboratory" title="U.S. Naval Research Laboratory">U.S. Naval Research Laboratory</a> have found a way to lessen the efficiency droop. They found that the droop arises from <a href="/wiki/Non-radiative_recombination" title="Non-radiative recombination">non-radiative</a> Auger recombination of the injected carriers. They created quantum wells with a soft confinement potential to lessen the non-radiative Auger processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Researchers at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taiwan_National_Central_University" title="Taiwan National Central University">Taiwan National Central University</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistar_Corp" title="Epistar Corp">Epistar Corp</a> are developing a way to lessen the efficiency droop by using ceramic aluminium nitride (AlN) substrates, which are more <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermally conductive</a> than the commercially used sapphire. The higher thermal conductivity reduces self-heating effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifetime_and_failure">Lifetime and failure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Lifetime and failure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_LED_failure_modes" title="List of LED failure modes">List of LED failure modes</a></div>
<p>Solid-state devices such as LEDs are subject to very limited <a href="/wiki/Wear_and_tear" title="Wear and tear">wear and tear</a> if operated at low currents and at low temperatures. Typical lifetimes quoted are 25,000 to 100,000 hours, but heat and current settings can extend or shorten this time significantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eere_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-eere-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most common symptom of LED (and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diode_laser" title="Diode laser">diode laser</a>) failure is the gradual lowering of light output and loss of efficiency. Sudden failures, although rare, can also occur. Early red LEDs were notable for their short service life. With the development of high-power LEDs, the devices are subjected to higher <a href="/wiki/Junction_temperature" title="Junction temperature">junction temperatures</a> and higher current densities than traditional devices. This causes stress on the material and may cause early light-output degradation. To quantitatively classify useful lifetime in a standardized manner it has been suggested to use L70 or L50, which are the runtimes (typically given in thousands of hours) at which a given LED reaches 70% and 50% of initial light output, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Whereas in most previous sources of light (incandescent lamps, discharge lamps, and those that burn combustible fuel, e.g. candles and oil lamps) the light results from heat, LEDs only operate if they are kept cool enough. The manufacturer commonly specifies a maximum junction temperature of 125 or 150 °C, and lower temperatures are advisable in the interests of long life. At these temperatures, relatively little heat is lost by radiation, which means that the light beam generated by an LED is cool.</p>
<p>The waste heat in a high-power LED (which as of 2015 can be less than half the power that it consumes) is conveyed by conduction through the substrate and package of the LED to a <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a>, which gives up the heat to the ambient air by convection. Careful thermal design is, therefore, essential, taking into account the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_resistance" title="Thermal resistance">thermal resistances</a> of the LED’s package, the heat sink and the interface between the two. Medium-power LEDs are often designed to be soldered directly to a <a href="/wiki/Printed_circuit_board" title="Printed circuit board">printed circuit board</a> that contains a thermally conductive metal layer. High-power LEDs are packaged in large-area ceramic packages designed to be attached to a metal <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a>, the interface being a material with high thermal conductivity (<a href="/wiki/Thermal_grease" title="Thermal grease">thermal grease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phase-change_material" title="Phase-change material">phase-change material</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermally_conductive_pad" title="Thermally conductive pad">thermally conductive pad</a> or <a href="/wiki/Thermal_adhesive" title="Thermal adhesive">thermal adhesive</a>).</p>
<p>If an LED-based lamp is installed in an unventilated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luminaire" title="Luminaire">luminaire</a>, or a luminaire is located in an environment that does not have free air circulation, the LED is likely to overheat, resulting in reduced life or early catastrophic failure. Thermal design is often based on an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambient_temperature" title="Ambient temperature">ambient temperature</a> of 25 °C (77 °F). LEDs used in outdoor applications, such as traffic signals or in-pavement signal lights, and in climates where the temperature within the light fixture gets very high, could experience reduced output or even failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPI.edu_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPI.edu-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since LED efficacy is higher at low temperatures, LED technology is well suited for supermarket <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freezer" title="Freezer">freezer</a> lighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Because LEDs produce less waste heat than incandescent lamps, their use in freezers can save on refrigeration costs as well. However, they may be more susceptible to frost and snow buildup than incandescent lamps,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPI.edu_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-RPI.edu-69">[69]</a></sup> so some LED lighting systems have been designed with an added heating circuit. Additionally, research has developed heat sink technologies that will transfer heat produced within the junction to appropriate areas of the light fixture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Colors_and_materials">Colors and materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Colors and materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_and_ultraviolet">Blue and ultraviolet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Blue and ultraviolet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_light_emitting_diodes_over_a_proto-board.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="1224" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Blue_light_emitting_diodes_over_a_proto-board.jpg/170px-Blue_light_emitting_diodes_over_a_proto-board.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Blue_light_emitting_diodes_over_a_proto-board.jpg/255px-Blue_light_emitting_diodes_over_a_proto-board.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Blue_light_emitting_diodes_over_a_proto-board.jpg/340px-Blue_light_emitting_diodes_over_a_proto-board.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_light_emitting_diodes_over_a_proto-board.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Blue" title="Blue">Blue</a> LEDs</div>
</div>
</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em">
<caption>External video</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herb_Maruska_original_blue_LED_College_of_New_Jersey_Sarnoff_Collection.png"><img alt="Herb Maruska original blue LED College of New Jersey Sarnoff Collection.png" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="1066" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Herb_Maruska_original_blue_LED_College_of_New_Jersey_Sarnoff_Collection.png/210px-Herb_Maruska_original_blue_LED_College_of_New_Jersey_Sarnoff_Collection.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Herb_Maruska_original_blue_LED_College_of_New_Jersey_Sarnoff_Collection.png/315px-Herb_Maruska_original_blue_LED_College_of_New_Jersey_Sarnoff_Collection.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Herb_Maruska_original_blue_LED_College_of_New_Jersey_Sarnoff_Collection.png/420px-Herb_Maruska_original_blue_LED_College_of_New_Jersey_Sarnoff_Collection.png 2x" width="210"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://vimeo.com/109205062" rel="nofollow">“The Original Blue LED”</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_Heritage_Foundation" title="Chemical Heritage Foundation">Chemical Heritage Foundation</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The first blue-violet LED using magnesium-doped gallium nitride was made at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> in 1972 by Herb Maruska and Wally Rhines, doctoral students in materials science and engineering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> At the time Maruska was on leave from <a href="/wiki/RCA" title="RCA">RCA Laboratories</a>, where he collaborated with Jacques Pankove on related work. In 1971, the year after Maruska left for Stanford, his RCA colleagues Pankove and Ed Miller demonstrated the first blue electroluminescence from zinc-doped gallium nitride, though the subsequent device Pankove and Miller built, the first actual gallium nitride light-emitting diode, emitted green light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In 1974 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Patent_Office" title="U.S. Patent Office">U.S. Patent Office</a> awarded Maruska, Rhines and Stanford professor David Stevenson a patent for their work in 1972 (U.S. Patent <a class="external text" href="http://www.google.com/patents/US3819974" rel="nofollow">US3819974 A</a>) and today magnesium-doping of gallium nitride continues to be the basis for all commercial blue LEDs and laser diodes. These devices built in the early 1970s had too little light output to be of practical use and research into gallium nitride devices slowed. In August 1989, Cree introduced the first commercially available blue LED based on the <a href="/wiki/Direct_and_indirect_band_gaps" title="Direct and indirect band gaps">indirect bandgap</a> semiconductor, silicon carbide (SiC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> SiC LEDs had very low efficiency, no more than about 0.03%, but did emit in the blue portion of the visible light spectrum.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In the late 1980s, key breakthroughs in GaN <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epitaxial" title="Epitaxial">epitaxial</a> growth and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-type_semiconductor" title="P-type semiconductor">p-type</a> doping<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> ushered in the modern era of GaN-based optoelectronic devices. Building upon this foundation, Theodore Moustakas at Boston University patented a method for producing high-brightness blue LEDs using a new two-step process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Two years later, in 1993, high-brightness blue LEDs were demonstrated again by <a href="/wiki/Shuji_Nakamura" title="Shuji Nakamura">Shuji Nakamura</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nichia_Corporation" title="Nichia Corporation">Nichia Corporation</a> using a gallium nitride growth process similar to Moustakas's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Both Moustakas and Nakamura were issued separate patents, which confused the issue of who was the original inventor (partly because although Moustakas invented his first, Nakamura filed first).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This new development revolutionized LED lighting, making high-power blue light sources practical, leading to the development of technologies like <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray" title="Blu-ray">Blu-ray</a>, as well as allowing the bright high-resolution screens of modern tablets and phones.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Nakamura was awarded the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Technology_Prize" title="Millennium Technology Prize">Millennium Technology Prize</a> for his invention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Nakamura, <a href="/wiki/Hiroshi_Amano" title="Hiroshi Amano">Hiroshi Amano</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isamu_Akasaki" title="Isamu Akasaki">Isamu Akasaki</a> were awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> in 2014 for the invention of the blue LED.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141007-DO_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141007-DO-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nobel2014_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-nobel2014-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In 2015, a US court ruled that three companies (i.e. the litigants who had not previously settled out of court) that had licensed Nakamura's patents for production in the United States had infringed Moustakas's prior patent, and ordered them to pay licensing fees of not less than 13 million USD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the late 1990s, blue LEDs became widely available. They have an active region consisting of one or more InGaN <a href="/wiki/Quantum_well" title="Quantum well">quantum wells</a> sandwiched between thicker layers of GaN, called cladding layers. By varying the relative In/Ga fraction in the InGaN quantum wells, the light emission can in theory be varied from violet to amber. <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_gallium_nitride" title="Aluminium gallium nitride">Aluminium gallium nitride</a> (AlGaN) of varying Al/Ga fraction can be used to manufacture the cladding and quantum well layers for ultraviolet LEDs, but these devices have not yet reached the level of efficiency and technological maturity of InGaN/GaN blue/green devices. If un-alloyed GaN is used in this case to form the active quantum well layers, the device will emit near-ultraviolet light with a peak wavelength centred around 365 nm. Green LEDs manufactured from the InGaN/GaN system are far more efficient and brighter than green LEDs produced with non-nitride material systems, but practical devices still exhibit efficiency too low for high-brightness applications.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>With nitrides containing aluminium, most often <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_gallium_nitride" title="Aluminium gallium nitride">AlGaN</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aluminium_gallium_indium_nitride&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aluminium gallium indium nitride (page does not exist)">AlGaInN</a>, even shorter wavelengths are achievable. Ultraviolet LEDs in a range of wavelengths are becoming available on the market. Near-UV emitters at wavelengths around 375–395 nm are already cheap and often encountered, for example, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_light" title="Black light">black light</a> lamp replacements for inspection of anti-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterfeiting" title="Counterfeiting">counterfeiting</a> UV watermarks in some documents and paper currencies. Shorter-wavelength diodes, while substantially more expensive, are commercially available for wavelengths down to 240 nm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> As the photosensitivity of microorganisms approximately matches the absorption spectrum of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, with a peak at about 260 nm, UV LED emitting at 250–270 nm are to be expected in prospective disinfection and sterilization devices. Recent research has shown that commercially available UVA LEDs (365 nm) are already effective disinfection and sterilization devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-water_sterilization_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-water_sterilization-96">[96]</a></sup> UV-C wavelengths were obtained in laboratories using <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_nitride" title="Aluminium nitride">aluminium nitride</a> (210 nm),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aln_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-aln-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Boron_nitride" title="Boron nitride">boron nitride</a> (215 nm)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BN_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-BN-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bn2_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-bn2-77">[77]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> (235 nm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dia_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-dia-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="RGB">RGB</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: RGB">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RGB-SMD-LED.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="731" data-file-width="731" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/RGB-SMD-LED.jpg/220px-RGB-SMD-LED.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/RGB-SMD-LED.jpg/330px-RGB-SMD-LED.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/RGB-SMD-LED.jpg/440px-RGB-SMD-LED.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RGB-SMD-LED.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
RGB-SMD-LED</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/RGB_color_model" title="RGB color model">RGB</a> LEDs consist of one red, one green, and one blue LED. By independently <a href="/wiki/Pulse-width_modulation" title="Pulse-width modulation">adjusting</a> each of the three, RGB LEDs are capable of producing a wide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color_gamut" title="Color gamut">color gamut</a>. Unlike dedicated-color LEDs, however, these obviously do not produce pure wavelengths. Moreover, such modules as commercially available are often not optimized for smooth color mixing.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="White">White</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: White">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are two primary ways of producing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_Light" title="Solar Light">white light</a>-emitting diodes (WLEDs), LEDs that generate high-intensity white light. One is to use individual LEDs that emit three <a href="/wiki/Primary_color" title="Primary color">primary colors</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>—red, green, and blue—and then mix all the colors to form white light. The other is to use a phosphor material to convert monochromatic light from a blue or UV LED to broad-spectrum white light, much in the same way a fluorescent light bulb works. It is important to note that the 'whiteness' of the light produced is essentially engineered to suit the human eye, and depending on the situation it may not always be appropriate to think of it as white light.</p>
<p>There are three main methods of mixing colors to produce white light from an LED:</p>
<ul>
<li>blue LED + green LED + red LED (color mixing; can be used as backlighting for displays, extremely poor for illumination due to gaps in spectrum)</li>
<li>near-UV or UV LED + RGB phosphor (an LED producing light with a wavelength shorter than blue's is used to excite an RGB phosphor)</li>
<li>blue LED + yellow phosphor (two complementary colors combine to form white light; more efficient than first two methods and more commonly used)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Because of <a href="/wiki/Metamerism_(color)" title="Metamerism (color)">metamerism</a>, it is possible to have quite different spectra that appear white. However, the appearance of objects illuminated by that light may vary as the spectrum varies, this is the issue of Colour rendition, quite separate from Colour Temperature, where a really orange or cyan object could appear with the wrong colour and much darker as the LED or phosphor does not emit the wavelength. The best colour rendition CFL and LEDs use a mix of phosphors, resulting in less efficiency but better quality of light. Though halogen is more orange Colour Temperature, it's still the best easily available artificial light source in terms of colour rendition.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="RGB_systems">RGB systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: RGB systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:382px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red-YellowGreen-Blue_LED_spectra.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="758" data-file-width="1073" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Red-YellowGreen-Blue_LED_spectra.png/380px-Red-YellowGreen-Blue_LED_spectra.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Red-YellowGreen-Blue_LED_spectra.png/570px-Red-YellowGreen-Blue_LED_spectra.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Red-YellowGreen-Blue_LED_spectra.png/760px-Red-YellowGreen-Blue_LED_spectra.png 2x" width="380"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red-YellowGreen-Blue_LED_spectra.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Combined spectral curves for blue, yellow-green, and high-brightness red solid-state semiconductor LEDs. <a href="/wiki/Full_width_at_half_maximum" title="Full width at half maximum">FWHM</a> spectral bandwidth is approximately 24–27 nm for all three colors.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RGB_LED.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1154" data-file-width="1731" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/RGB_LED.jpg/220px-RGB_LED.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/RGB_LED.jpg/330px-RGB_LED.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/RGB_LED.jpg/440px-RGB_LED.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RGB_LED.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
RGB LED</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>White light can be formed by mixing differently colored lights; the most common method is to use red, green, and blue (RGB). Hence the method is called multi-color white LEDs (sometimes referred to as RGB LEDs). Because these need electronic circuits to control the blending and <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffusion</a> of different colors, and because the individual color LEDs typically have slightly different emission patterns (leading to variation of the color depending on direction) even if they are made as a single unit, these are seldom used to produce white lighting. Nonetheless, this method has many applications because of the flexibility of mixing different colors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> and in principle, this mechanism also has higher quantum efficiency in producing white light.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>There are several types of multi-color white LEDs: <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dichromatic" title="wiktionary:dichromatic">di-</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trichromatic" title="Trichromatic">tri-</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrachromatic" title="Tetrachromatic">tetrachromatic</a> white LEDs. Several key factors that play among these different methods include color stability, <a href="/wiki/Color_rendering_index" title="Color rendering index">color rendering</a> capability, and luminous efficacy. Often, higher efficiency will mean lower color rendering, presenting a trade-off between the luminous efficacy and color rendering. For example, the dichromatic white LEDs have the best luminous efficacy (120 lm/W), but the lowest color rendering capability. However, although tetrachromatic white LEDs have excellent color rendering capability, they often have poor luminous efficacy. Trichromatic white LEDs are in between, having both good luminous efficacy (&gt;70 lm/W) and fair color rendering capability.</p>
<p>One of the challenges is the development of more efficient green LEDs. The theoretical maximum for green LEDs is 683 lumens per watt but as of 2010 few green LEDs exceed even 100 lumens per watt. The blue and red LEDs get closer to their theoretical limits.</p>
<p>Multi-color LEDs offer not merely another means to form white light but a new means to form light of different colors. Most <a href="/wiki/Color#Perception" title="Color">perceivable colors</a> can be formed by mixing different amounts of three primary colors. This allows precise dynamic color control. As more effort is devoted to investigating this method, multi-color LEDs should have profound influence on the fundamental method that we use to produce and control light color. However, before this type of LED can play a role on the market, several technical problems must be solved. These include that this type of LED's emission power <a href="/wiki/Exponential_decay" title="Exponential decay">decays exponentially</a> with rising temperature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> resulting in a substantial change in color stability. Such problems inhibit and may preclude industrial use. Thus, many new package designs aimed at solving this problem have been proposed and their results are now being reproduced by researchers and scientists. However multi-colour LEDs without phosphors can never provide good quality lighting because each LED is a narrow band source (see graph).
