LEDs without phosphor while a poorer solution for general lighting are the best solution for displays, either backlight of LCD, or direct LED based pixels.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Correlated_color_temperature" title="Correlated color temperature">Correlated color temperature</a> (CCT) dimming for LED technology is regarded as a difficult task since binning, age and temperature drift effects of LEDs change the actual color value output. Feedback loop systems are used for example with color sensors, to actively monitor and control the color output of multiple color mixing LEDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phosphor-based_LEDs">Phosphor-based LEDs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Phosphor-based LEDs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:White_LED.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2459" data-file-width="3423" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/White_LED.png/350px-White_LED.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/White_LED.png/525px-White_LED.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/White_LED.png/700px-White_LED.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:White_LED.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spectrum of a white LED showing blue light directly emitted by the GaN-based LED (peak at about 465 nm) and the more broadband <a href="/wiki/Stokes_shift" title="Stokes shift">Stokes-shifted</a> light emitted by the Ce<sup>3+</sup>:YAG phosphor, which emits at roughly 500–700 nm</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>This method involves <a href="/wiki/Coating" title="Coating">coating</a> LEDs of one color (mostly blue LEDs made of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/InGaN" title="InGaN">InGaN</a>) with <a href="/wiki/Phosphor" title="Phosphor">phosphors</a> of different colors to form white light; the resultant LEDs are called phosphor-based or phosphor-converted white LEDs (pcLEDs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> A fraction of the blue light undergoes the <a href="/wiki/Stokes_shift" title="Stokes shift">Stokes shift</a> being transformed from shorter wavelengths to longer. Depending on the color of the original LED, phosphors of different colors can be employed. If several phosphor layers of distinct colors are applied, the emitted spectrum is broadened, effectively raising the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color_Rendering_Index" title="Color Rendering Index">color rendering index</a> (CRI) value of a given LED.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Phosphor-based LED efficiency losses are due to the heat loss from the Stokes shift and also other phosphor-related degradation issues. Their luminous efficacies compared to normal LEDs depend on the spectral distribution of the resultant light output and the original wavelength of the LED itself. For example, the luminous efficacy of a typical YAG yellow phosphor based white LED ranges from 3 to 5 times the luminous efficacy of the original blue LED because of the human eye's greater sensitivity to yellow than to blue (as modeled in the <a href="/wiki/Luminosity_function" title="Luminosity function">luminosity function</a>). Due to the simplicity of manufacturing, the phosphor method is still the most popular method for making high-intensity white LEDs. The design and production of a light source or light fixture using a monochrome emitter with phosphor conversion is simpler and cheaper than a complex <a href="#RGB_systems">RGB</a> system, and the majority of high-intensity white LEDs presently on the market are manufactured using phosphor light conversion.</p>
<p>Among the challenges being faced to improve the efficiency of LED-based white light sources is the development of more efficient phosphors. As of 2010, the most efficient yellow phosphor is still the YAG phosphor, with less than 10% Stokes shift loss. Losses attributable to internal optical losses due to re-absorption in the LED chip and in the LED packaging itself account typically for another 10% to 30% of efficiency loss. Currently, in the area of phosphor LED development, much effort is being spent on optimizing these devices to higher light output and higher operation temperatures. For instance, the efficiency can be raised by adapting better package design or by using a more suitable type of phosphor. Conformal coating process is frequently used to address the issue of varying phosphor thickness.</p>
<p>Some phosphor-based white LEDs encapsulate InGaN blue LEDs inside phosphor-coated epoxy. Alternatively, the LED might be paired with a remote phosphor, a preformed polycarbonate piece coated with the phosphor material. Remote phosphors provide more diffuse light, which is desirable for many applications. Remote phosphor designs are also more tolerant of variations in the LED emissions spectrum. A common yellow phosphor material is <a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">cerium</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doping_(Semiconductors)" title="Doping (Semiconductors)">doped</a> <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_aluminium_garnet" title="Yttrium aluminium garnet">yttrium aluminium garnet</a> (Ce<sup>3+</sup>:YAG).</p>
<p>White LEDs can also be made by <a href="/wiki/Coating" title="Coating">coating</a> near-<a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> (NUV) LEDs with a mixture of high-efficiency <a href="/wiki/Europium" title="Europium">europium</a>-based phosphors that emit red and blue, plus copper and aluminium-doped zinc sulfide (ZnS:Cu, Al) that emits green. This is a method analogous to the way <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamps</a> work. This method is less efficient than blue LEDs with YAG:Ce phosphor, as the <a href="/wiki/Stokes_shift" title="Stokes shift">Stokes shift</a> is larger, so more energy is converted to heat, but yields light with better spectral characteristics, which render color better. Due to the higher radiative output of the ultraviolet LEDs than of the blue ones, both methods offer comparable brightness. A concern is that UV light may leak from a malfunctioning light source and cause harm to human eyes or skin.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_white_LEDs">Other white LEDs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other white LEDs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Another method used to produce experimental white light LEDs used no phosphors at all and was based on <a href="/wiki/Epitaxy" title="Epitaxy">homoepitaxially</a> grown <a href="/wiki/Zinc_selenide" title="Zinc selenide">zinc selenide</a> (ZnSe) on a ZnSe substrate that simultaneously emitted blue light from its active region and yellow light from the substrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>A new style of wafers composed of gallium-nitride-on-silicon (GaN-on-Si) is being used to produce white LEDs using 200-mm silicon wafers. This avoids the typical costly <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">sapphire</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substrate_(materials_science)" title="Substrate (materials science)">substrate</a> in relatively small 100- or 150-mm wafer sizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-electronicdesign.com_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-electronicdesign.com-105">[105]</a></sup> The sapphire apparatus must be coupled with a mirror-like collector to reflect light that would otherwise be wasted. It is predicted that by 2020, 40% of all GaN LEDs will be made with GaN-on-Si. Manufacturing large sapphire material is difficult, while large silicon material is cheaper and more abundant. LED companies shifting from using sapphire to silicon should be a minimal investment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_light-emitting_diodes_.28OLEDs.29">Organic light-emitting diodes (OLEDs)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Organic light-emitting diodes (OLEDs)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_light-emitting_diode" title="Organic light-emitting diode">Organic light-emitting diode</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OLED_EarlyProduct.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="480" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/OLED_EarlyProduct.JPG/220px-OLED_EarlyProduct.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/OLED_EarlyProduct.JPG/330px-OLED_EarlyProduct.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/OLED_EarlyProduct.JPG/440px-OLED_EarlyProduct.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OLED_EarlyProduct.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Demonstration of a <a href="/wiki/Flexible_organic_light-emitting_diode" title="Flexible organic light-emitting diode">flexible OLED</a> device</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orange_LED_emitting.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="765" data-file-width="1148" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Orange_LED_emitting.png/220px-Orange_LED_emitting.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Orange_LED_emitting.png/330px-Orange_LED_emitting.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Orange_LED_emitting.png/440px-Orange_LED_emitting.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orange_LED_emitting.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Orange light-emitting diode</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In an organic light-emitting diode (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_light-emitting_diode" title="Organic light-emitting diode">OLED</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Electroluminescence" title="Electroluminescence">electroluminescent</a> material comprising the emissive layer of the diode is an <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compound</a>. The organic material is electrically conductive due to the <a href="/wiki/Delocalized_electron" title="Delocalized electron">delocalization</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pi_bond" title="Pi bond">pi electrons</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Conjugated_system" title="Conjugated system">conjugation</a> over all or part of the molecule, and the material therefore functions as an <a href="/wiki/Organic_semiconductor" title="Organic semiconductor">organic semiconductor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The organic materials can be small organic <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystalline</a> <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">phase</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OLEDSolidState_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-OLEDSolidState-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>The potential advantages of OLEDs include thin, low-cost displays with a low driving voltage, wide viewing angle, and high contrast and color gamut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bardsley_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-bardsley-109">[109]</a></sup> Polymer LEDs have the added benefit of printable and <a href="/wiki/Flexible_organic_light-emitting_diode" title="Flexible organic light-emitting diode">flexible</a> displays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> OLEDs have been used to make visual displays for portable electronic devices such as cellphones, digital cameras, and MP3 players while possible future uses include lighting and televisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OLEDSolidState_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-OLEDSolidState-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bardsley_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-bardsley-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_dot_LEDs">Quantum dot LEDs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Quantum dot LEDs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot" title="Quantum dot">Quantum dots</a> (QD) are semiconductor <a href="/wiki/Nanocrystal" title="Nanocrystal">nanocrystals</a> whose optical properties allow their emission color to be tuned from the visible into the infrared spectrum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MITqdot2002_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-MITqdot2002-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> This allows quantum dot LEDs to create almost any color on the <a href="/wiki/International_Commission_on_Illumination" title="International Commission on Illumination">CIE</a> diagram. This provides more color options and better color rendering than white LEDs since the emission spectrum is much narrower, characteristic of quantum confined states.</p>
<p>There are two types of schemes for QD excitation. One uses photo excitation with a primary light source LED (typically blue or UV LEDs are used). The other is direct electrical excitation first demonstrated by Alivisatos et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>One example of the photo-excitation scheme is a method developed by Michael Bowers, at <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University" title="Vanderbilt University">Vanderbilt University</a> in Nashville, involving coating a blue LED with quantum dots that glow white in response to the blue light from the LED. This method emits a warm, yellowish-white light similar to that made by <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">incandescent light bulbs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Quantum dots are also being considered for use in white light-emitting diodes in liquid crystal display (LCD) televisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2011 scientists at PlasmaChem GmbH were able to synthesize quantum dots for LED applications and build a light converter on their basis, which was able to efficiently convert light from blue to any other color for many hundred hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Such QDs can be used to emit visible or near infrared light of any wavelength being excited by light with a shorter wavelength.</p>
<p>The structure of QD-LEDs used for the electrical-excitation scheme is similar to basic design of <a href="/wiki/OLED" title="OLED">OLEDs</a>. A layer of quantum dots is sandwiched between layers of electron-transporting and hole-transporting materials. An applied electric field causes electrons and holes to move into the quantum dot layer and recombine forming an <a href="/wiki/Exciton" title="Exciton">exciton</a> that excites a QD. This scheme is commonly studied for <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot_display" title="Quantum dot display">quantum dot display</a>. The tunability of emission wavelengths and narrow bandwidth is also beneficial as excitation sources for fluorescence imaging. Fluorescence near-field scanning optical microscopy (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSOM" title="NSOM">NSOM</a>) utilizing an integrated QD-LED has been demonstrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2008, a luminous efficacy of 300 <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">lumens</a> of visible light per watt of <a href="/wiki/Radiant_flux" title="Radiant flux">radiation</a> (not per electrical watt) and warm-light emission was achieved by using <a href="/wiki/Nanocrystal" title="Nanocrystal">nanocrystals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Verschiedene_LEDs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1193" data-file-width="3392" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Verschiedene_LEDs.jpg/750px-Verschiedene_LEDs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Verschiedene_LEDs.jpg/1125px-Verschiedene_LEDs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Verschiedene_LEDs.jpg/1500px-Verschiedene_LEDs.jpg 2x" width="750"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Verschiedene_LEDs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
LEDs are produced in a variety of shapes and sizes. The color of the plastic lens is often the same as the actual color of light emitted, but not always. For instance, purple plastic is often used for <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> LEDs, and most blue devices have colorless housings. Modern high-power LEDs such as those used for lighting and backlighting are generally found in <a href="/wiki/Surface-mount_technology" title="Surface-mount technology">surface-mount technology</a> (SMT) packages (not shown).</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The main types of LEDs are miniature, high-power devices and custom designs such as alphanumeric or multi-color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miniature">Miniature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Miniature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Single_and_multicolor_surface_mount_miniature_LEDs_in_most_common_sizes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2601" data-file-width="3648" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Single_and_multicolor_surface_mount_miniature_LEDs_in_most_common_sizes.jpg/220px-Single_and_multicolor_surface_mount_miniature_LEDs_in_most_common_sizes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Single_and_multicolor_surface_mount_miniature_LEDs_in_most_common_sizes.jpg/330px-Single_and_multicolor_surface_mount_miniature_LEDs_in_most_common_sizes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Single_and_multicolor_surface_mount_miniature_LEDs_in_most_common_sizes.jpg/440px-Single_and_multicolor_surface_mount_miniature_LEDs_in_most_common_sizes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Single_and_multicolor_surface_mount_miniature_LEDs_in_most_common_sizes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photo of miniature <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_mount" title="Surface mount">surface mount</a> LEDs in most common sizes. They can be much smaller than a traditional 5 mm lamp type LED which is shown on the upper left corner.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Very_small_1.6x1.6x0.35_mm_RGB_Surface_Mount_LED_EAST1616RGBA2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="774" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Very_small_1.6x1.6x0.35_mm_RGB_Surface_Mount_LED_EAST1616RGBA2.jpg/220px-Very_small_1.6x1.6x0.35_mm_RGB_Surface_Mount_LED_EAST1616RGBA2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Very_small_1.6x1.6x0.35_mm_RGB_Surface_Mount_LED_EAST1616RGBA2.jpg/330px-Very_small_1.6x1.6x0.35_mm_RGB_Surface_Mount_LED_EAST1616RGBA2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Very_small_1.6x1.6x0.35_mm_RGB_Surface_Mount_LED_EAST1616RGBA2.jpg/440px-Very_small_1.6x1.6x0.35_mm_RGB_Surface_Mount_LED_EAST1616RGBA2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Very_small_1.6x1.6x0.35_mm_RGB_Surface_Mount_LED_EAST1616RGBA2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Very small (1.6x1.6x0.35 mm) red, green, and blue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_mount" title="Surface mount">surface mount</a> miniature LED package with gold <a href="/wiki/Wire_bonding" title="Wire bonding">wire bonding</a> details.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>These are mostly single-die LEDs used as indicators, and they come in various sizes from 2 mm to 8 mm, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Through-hole" title="Through-hole">through-hole</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_mount" title="Surface mount">surface mount</a> packages. They usually do not use a separate <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Typical current ratings range from around 1 mA to above 20 mA. The small size sets a natural upper boundary on power consumption due to heat caused by the high current density and need for a heat sink. Often daisy chained as used in <a href="/wiki/LED_strip_light" title="LED strip light">LED tapes</a>.</p>
<p>Common package shapes include round, with a domed or flat top, rectangular with a flat top (as used in bar-graph displays), and triangular or square with a flat top. The encapsulation may also be clear or tinted to improve contrast and viewing angle.</p>
<p>Researchers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">University of Washington</a> have invented the thinnest LED. It is made of <a href="/wiki/Two-dimensional_space" title="Two-dimensional space">two-dimensional (2-D)</a> flexible materials. It is three <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> thick, which is 10 to 20 times thinner than <a href="/wiki/Three-dimensional_space" title="Three-dimensional space">three-dimensional (3-D)</a> LEDs and is also 10,000 times smaller than the thickness of a human hair. These 2-D LEDs are going to make it possible to create smaller, more energy-efficient lighting, <a href="/wiki/Optical_communication" title="Optical communication">optical communication</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nano_lasers" title="Nano lasers">nano lasers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are three main categories of miniature single die LEDs:</p>
<dl>
<dt>Low-current</dt>
<dd>Typically rated for 2 mA at around 2 V (approximately 4 mW consumption)</dd>
<dt>Standard</dt>
<dd>20 mA LEDs (ranging from approximately 40 mW to 90 mW) at around:
<ul>
<li>1.9 to 2.1 V for red, orange, yellow, and traditional green</li>
<li>3.0 to 3.4 V for pure green and blue</li>
<li>2.9 to 4.2 V for violet, pink, purple and white</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Ultra-high-output</dt>
<dd>20 mA at approximately 2 or 4–5 V, designed for viewing in direct sunlight</dd>
</dl>
<p>5 V and 12 V LEDs are ordinary miniature LEDs that incorporate a suitable <a href="/wiki/Series_and_parallel_circuits#Resistors" title="Series and parallel circuits">series</a> <a href="/wiki/Resistor" title="Resistor">resistor</a> for direct connection to a 5 V or 12 V supply.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High-power">High-power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: High-power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2007-07-24_High-power_light_emitting_diodes_(Luxeon,_Lumiled).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/2007-07-24_High-power_light_emitting_diodes_%28Luxeon%2C_Lumiled%29.jpg/220px-2007-07-24_High-power_light_emitting_diodes_%28Luxeon%2C_Lumiled%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/2007-07-24_High-power_light_emitting_diodes_%28Luxeon%2C_Lumiled%29.jpg/330px-2007-07-24_High-power_light_emitting_diodes_%28Luxeon%2C_Lumiled%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/2007-07-24_High-power_light_emitting_diodes_%28Luxeon%2C_Lumiled%29.jpg/440px-2007-07-24_High-power_light_emitting_diodes_%28Luxeon%2C_Lumiled%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2007-07-24_High-power_light_emitting_diodes_(Luxeon,_Lumiled).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
High-power light-emitting diodes attached to an LED star base (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Luxeon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Luxeon (page does not exist)">Luxeon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philips_Lumileds_Lighting_Company" title="Philips Lumileds Lighting Company">Lumileds</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_lighting" title="Solid-state lighting">Solid-state lighting</a>, <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">LED lamp</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_management_of_high-power_LEDs" title="Thermal management of high-power LEDs">Thermal management of high-power LEDs</a></div>
<p>High-power LEDs (HP-LEDs) or high-output LEDs (HO-LEDs) can be driven at currents from hundreds of mA to more than an ampere, compared with the tens of mA for other LEDs. Some can emit over a thousand lumens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> LED <a href="/wiki/Power_density" title="Power density">power densities</a> up to 300 W/cm<sup>2</sup> have been achieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infiniled.com_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-infiniled.com-126">[126]</a></sup> Since overheating is destructive, the HP-LEDs must be mounted on a heat sink to allow for heat dissipation. If the heat from an HP-LED is not removed, the device will fail in seconds. One HP-LED can often replace an incandescent bulb in a <a href="/wiki/Flashlight" title="Flashlight">flashlight</a>, or be set in an array to form a powerful <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">LED lamp</a>.</p>
<p>Some well-known HP-LEDs in this category are the Nichia 19 series, Lumileds Rebel Led, Osram Opto Semiconductors Golden Dragon, and Cree X-lamp. As of September 2009, some HP-LEDs manufactured by Cree now exceed 105 lm/W.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xlamp_Xp-G_Led_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xlamp_Xp-G_Led-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Examples for <a href="/wiki/Haitz%27s_law" title="Haitz's law">Haitz's law</a>, which predicts an exponential rise in light output and efficacy of LEDs over time, are the CREE XP-G series LED which achieved 105 lm/W in 2009<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xlamp_Xp-G_Led_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Xlamp_Xp-G_Led-127">[127]</a></sup> and the Nichia 19 series with a typical efficacy of 140 lm/W, released in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="AC_driven">AC driven</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: AC driven">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>LEDs have been developed by Seoul Semiconductor that can operate on AC power without the need for a DC converter. For each half-cycle, part of the LED emits light and part is dark, and this is reversed during the next half-cycle. The efficacy of this type of HP-LED is typically 40 lm/W.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> A large number of LED elements in series may be able to operate directly from line voltage. In 2009, Seoul Semiconductor released a high DC voltage LED, named as 'Acrich MJT', capable of being driven from AC power with a simple controlling circuit. The low-power dissipation of these LEDs affords them more flexibility than the original AC LED design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDA_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-IDA-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Application-specific_variations">Application-specific variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Application-specific variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flashing">Flashing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Flashing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Flashing LEDs are used as attention seeking indicators without requiring external electronics. Flashing LEDs resemble standard LEDs but they contain an integrated <a href="/wiki/Multivibrator" title="Multivibrator">multivibrator</a> circuit that causes the LED to flash with a typical period of one second. In diffused lens LEDs, this circuit is visible as a small black dot. Most flashing LEDs emit light of one color, but more sophisticated devices can flash between multiple colors and even fade through a color sequence using RGB color mixing.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bi-color">Bi-color</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Bi-color">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Bi-color LEDs contain two different LED emitters in one case. There are two types of these. One type consists of two dies connected to the same two leads <a href="/wiki/Antiparallel_(electronics)" title="Antiparallel (electronics)">antiparallel</a> to each other. Current flow in one direction emits one color, and current in the opposite direction emits the other color. The other type consists of two dies with separate leads for both dies and another lead for common anode or cathode so that they can be controlled independently. The most common bi-color combination is red/traditional green, however, other available combinations include amber/traditional green, red/pure green, red/blue, and blue/pure green.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tri-color">Tri-color</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Tri-color">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Tri-color LEDs contain three different LED emitters in one case. Each emitter is connected to a separate lead so they can be controlled independently. A four-lead arrangement is typical with one common lead (anode or cathode) and an additional lead for each color.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="RGB_2">RGB</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: RGB">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>RGB LEDs are tri-color LEDs with red, green, and blue emitters, in general using a four-wire connection with one common lead (anode or cathode). These LEDs can have either common positive or common negative leads. Others, however, have only two leads (positive and negative) and have a built-in tiny <a href="/wiki/Electronic_control_unit" title="Electronic control unit">electronic control unit</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Decorative-multicolor">Decorative-multicolor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Decorative-multicolor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Decorative-multicolor LEDs incorporate several emitters of different colors supplied by only two lead-out wires. Colors are switched internally by varying the supply voltage.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alphanumeric">Alphanumeric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Alphanumeric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Alphanumeric LEDs are available in <a href="/wiki/Seven-segment_display" title="Seven-segment display">seven-segment</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starburst_display" title="Starburst display">starburst</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dot-matrix_display" title="Dot-matrix display">dot-matrix</a> format. Seven-segment displays handle all numbers and a limited set of letters. Starburst displays can display all letters. Dot-matrix displays typically use 5x7 pixels per character. Seven-segment LED displays were in widespread use in the 1970s and 1980s, but rising use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_crystal_display" title="Liquid crystal display">liquid crystal displays</a>, with their lower power needs and greater display flexibility, has reduced the popularity of numeric and alphanumeric LED displays.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital-RGB">Digital-RGB</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Digital-RGB">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Digital-RGB LEDs are RGB LEDs that contain their own "smart" control electronics. In addition to power and ground, these provide connections for data-in, data-out, and sometimes a clock or strobe signal. These are connected in a <a href="/wiki/Daisy_chain_(electrical_engineering)" title="Daisy chain (electrical engineering)">daisy chain</a>, with the data in of the first LED sourced by a microprocessor, which can control the brightness and color of each LED independently of the others. They are used where a combination of maximum control and minimum visible electronics are needed such as strings for Christmas and LED matrices. Some even have refresh rates in the kHz range, allowing for basic video applications.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Filament">Filament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Filament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/LED_filament" title="LED filament">LED filament</a> consists of multiple LED chips connected in series on a common longitudinal substrate that forms a thin rod reminiscent of a traditional incandescent filament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> These are being used as a low-cost decorative alternative for traditional light bulbs that are being phased out in many countries. The filaments require a rather high voltage to light to nominal brightness, allowing them to work efficiently and simply with mains voltages. Often a simple rectifier and capacitive current limiting are employed to create a low-cost replacement for a traditional light bulb without the complexity of creating a low voltage, high current converter which is required by single die LEDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Usually, they are packaged in a sealed enclosure with a shape similar to lamps they were designed to replace (e.g. a bulb) and filled with inert nitrogen or carbon dioxide gas to remove heat efficiently.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Considerations_for_use">Considerations for use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Considerations for use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_sources">Power sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Power sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED_power_sources" title="LED power sources">LED power sources</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LED_circuit.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="100" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/LED_circuit.svg/170px-LED_circuit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/LED_circuit.svg/255px-LED_circuit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/LED_circuit.svg/340px-LED_circuit.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LED_circuit.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Simple LED circuit with resistor for current limiting</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The current–voltage characteristic of an LED is similar to other diodes, in that the current is dependent exponentially on the voltage (see <a href="/wiki/Shockley_diode_equation" title="Shockley diode equation">Shockley diode equation</a>). This means that a small change in voltage can cause a large change in current.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> If the applied voltage exceeds the LED's forward voltage drop by a small amount, the current rating may be exceeded by a large amount, potentially damaging or destroying the LED. The typical solution is to use <a href="/wiki/Constant_current" title="Constant current">constant-current</a> power supplies to keep the current below the LED's maximum current rating. Since most common power sources (batteries, mains) are constant-voltage sources, most LED fixtures must include a power converter, at least a current-limiting resistor. However, the high resistance of three-volt <a href="/wiki/Button_cell" title="Button cell">coin cells</a> combined with the high differential resistance of nitride-based LEDs makes it possible to power such an LED from such a coin cell without an external resistor.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_polarity">Electrical polarity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Electrical polarity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_polarity_of_LEDs" title="Electrical polarity of LEDs">Electrical polarity of LEDs</a></div>
<p>As with all diodes, current flows easily from p-type to n-type material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> However, no current flows and no light is emitted if a small voltage is applied in the reverse direction. If the reverse voltage grows large enough to exceed the <a href="/wiki/Breakdown_voltage" title="Breakdown voltage">breakdown voltage</a>, a large current flows and the LED may be damaged. If the reverse current is sufficiently limited to avoid damage, the reverse-conducting LED is a useful <a href="/wiki/Hardware_random_number_generator#Physical_phenomena_without_quantum-random_properties" title="Hardware random number generator">noise diode</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_and_health">Safety and health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Safety and health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The vast majority of devices containing LEDs are "safe under all conditions of normal use", and so are classified as "Class 1 LED product"/"LED Klasse 1". At present, only a few LEDs—extremely bright LEDs that also have a tightly focused viewing angle of 8° or less—could, in theory, cause temporary blindness, and so are classified as "Class 2".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The opinion of the French Agency for Food, Environmental and Occupational Health &amp; Safety (ANSES) of 2010, on the health issues concerning LEDs, suggested banning public use of lamps which were in the moderate Risk Group 2, especially those with a high blue component in places frequented by children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> In general, <a href="/wiki/Laser_safety" title="Laser safety">laser safety</a> regulations—and the "Class 1", "Class 2", etc. system—also apply to LEDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>While LEDs have the advantage over <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamps</a> that they do not contain <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, they may contain other hazardous metals such as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>. Regarding the toxicity of LEDs when treated as waste, a study published in 2011 stated: "According to federal standards, LEDs are not hazardous except for low-intensity red LEDs, which leached Pb [lead] at levels exceeding regulatory limits (186 mg/L; regulatory limit: 5). However, according to California regulations, excessive levels of copper (up to 3892 mg/kg; limit: 2500), lead (up to 8103 mg/kg; limit: 1000), <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> (up to 4797 mg/kg; limit: 2000), or <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> (up to 721 mg/kg; limit: 500) render all except low-intensity yellow LEDs hazardous."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Limetal2011_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Limetal2011-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advantages">Advantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Advantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b>Efficiency:</b> LEDs emit more lumens per watt than incandescent light bulbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The efficiency of LED lighting fixtures is not affected by shape and size, unlike fluorescent light bulbs or tubes.</li>
<li><b>Color:</b> LEDs can emit light of an intended color without using any color filters as traditional lighting methods need. This is more efficient and can lower initial costs.</li>
<li><b>Size:</b> LEDs can be very small (smaller than 2 mm<sup>2</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>) and are easily attached to printed circuit boards.</li>
<li><b>Warmup time:</b> LEDs light up very quickly. A typical red indicator LED will achieve full brightness in under a <a href="/wiki/Microsecond" title="Microsecond">microsecond</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> LEDs used in communications devices can have even faster response times.</li>
<li><b>Cycling:</b> LEDs are ideal for uses subject to frequent on-off cycling, unlike incandescent and fluorescent lamps that fail faster when cycled often, or <a href="/wiki/High-intensity_discharge_lamp" title="High-intensity discharge lamp">high-intensity discharge lamps</a> (HID lamps) that require a long time before restarting.</li>
<li><b>Dimming:</b> LEDs can very easily be <a href="/wiki/Dimmer" title="Dimmer">dimmed</a> either by <a href="/wiki/Pulse-width_modulation" title="Pulse-width modulation">pulse-width modulation</a> or lowering the forward current.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> This pulse-width modulation is why LED lights, particularly headlights on cars, when viewed on camera or by some people, appear to be flashing or flickering. This is a type of <a href="/wiki/Stroboscopic_effect" title="Stroboscopic effect">stroboscopic effect</a>.</li>
<li><b>Cool light:</b> In contrast to most light sources, LEDs radiate very little heat in the form of <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">IR</a> that can cause damage to sensitive objects or fabrics. Wasted energy is dispersed as heat through the base of the LED.</li>
<li><b>Slow failure:</b> LEDs mostly fail by dimming over time, rather than the abrupt failure of incandescent bulbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eere_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-eere-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Lifetime:</b> LEDs can have a relatively long useful life. One report estimates 35,000 to 50,000 hours of useful life, though time to complete failure may be longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Fluorescent tubes typically are rated at about 10,000 to 15,000 hours, depending partly on the conditions of use, and incandescent light bulbs at 1,000 to 2,000 hours. Several <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">DOE</a> demonstrations have shown that reduced maintenance costs from this extended lifetime, rather than energy savings, is the primary factor in determining the payback period for an LED product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Shock resistance:</b> LEDs, being solid-state components, are difficult to damage with external shock, unlike fluorescent and incandescent bulbs, which are fragile.</li>
<li><b>Focus:</b> The solid package of the LED can be designed to <a href="/wiki/Focus_(optics)" title="Focus (optics)">focus</a> its light. Incandescent and fluorescent sources often require an external reflector to collect light and direct it in a usable manner. For larger LED packages <a href="/wiki/Total_internal_reflection" title="Total internal reflection">total internal reflection</a> (TIR) lenses are often used to the same effect. However, when large quantities of light are needed many light sources are usually deployed, which are difficult to focus or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collimate" title="Collimate">collimate</a> towards the same target.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disadvantages">Disadvantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Disadvantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b>Initial price:</b> LEDs are currently slightly more expensive (price per lumen) on an initial capital cost basis, than other lighting technologies. As of March 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, at least one manufacturer claims to have reached $1 per kilolumen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The additional expense partially stems from the relatively low lumen output and the drive circuitry and power supplies needed.</li>
<li><b>Temperature dependence:</b> LED performance largely depends on the ambient temperature of the operating environment – or thermal management properties. Overdriving an LED in high ambient temperatures may result in overheating the LED package, eventually leading to device failure. An adequate <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a> is needed to maintain long life. This is especially important in automotive, medical, and military uses where devices must operate over a wide range of temperatures, which require low failure rates. Toshiba has produced LEDs with an operating temperature range of −40 to 100 °C, which suits the LEDs for both indoor and outdoor use in applications such as lamps, ceiling lighting, street lights, and floodlights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-electronicdesign.com_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-electronicdesign.com-105">[105]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Voltage sensitivity:</b> LEDs must be supplied with a voltage above their <a href="/wiki/P%E2%80%93n_junction#Forward_bias" title="P–n junction">threshold voltage</a> and a current below their rating. Current and lifetime change greatly with a small change in applied voltage. They thus require a current-regulated supply (usually just a series resistor for indicator LEDs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Color rendition:</b> Most cool-<a href="#Other_white_LEDs">white LEDs</a> have spectra that differ significantly from a <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black body</a> radiator like the sun or an incandescent light. The spike at 460 nm and dip at 500 nm can cause the color of objects to be <a href="/wiki/Color_vision" title="Color vision">perceived differently</a> under cool-white LED illumination than sunlight or incandescent sources, due to <a href="/wiki/Metamerism_(color)" title="Metamerism (color)">metamerism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> red surfaces being rendered particularly poorly by typical phosphor-based cool-white LEDs.</li>
<li><b>Area light source:</b> Single LEDs do not approximate a <a href="/wiki/Point_source" title="Point source">point source</a> of light giving a spherical light distribution, but rather a <a href="/wiki/Lambert%27s_cosine_law" title="Lambert's cosine law">lambertian</a> distribution. So LEDs are difficult to apply to uses needing a spherical light field; however, different fields of light can be manipulated by the application of different optics or "lenses". LEDs cannot provide divergence below a few degrees. In contrast, lasers can emit beams with divergences of 0.2 degrees or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Electrical_polarity" title="Electrical polarity">Electrical polarity</a>:</b> Unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incandescent" title="Incandescent">incandescent</a> light bulbs, which illuminate regardless of the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_polarity" title="Electrical polarity">electrical polarity</a>, LEDs will only light with correct electrical polarity. To automatically match source polarity to LED devices, <a href="/wiki/Rectifier" title="Rectifier">rectifiers</a> can be used.</li>
<li><b>Blue hazard:</b> There is a concern that <a href="#Ultraviolet_and_blue_LEDs">blue LEDs</a> and cool-white LEDs are now capable of exceeding safe limits of the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue-light_hazard" title="Blue-light hazard">blue-light hazard</a> as defined in eye safety specifications such as ANSI/IESNA RP-27.1–05: Recommended Practice for Photobiological Safety for Lamp and Lamp Systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlueLEDAhealthHazard_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlueLEDAhealthHazard-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Light_pollution" title="Light pollution">Light pollution</a>:</b> Because <a href="#White_light">white LEDs</a>, especially those with high <a href="/wiki/Color_temperature" title="Color temperature">color temperature</a>, emit much more short wavelength light than conventional outdoor light sources such as high-pressure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_vapor_lamp" title="Sodium vapor lamp">sodium vapor lamps</a>, the increased blue and green sensitivity of <a href="/wiki/Scotopic_vision" title="Scotopic vision">scotopic vision</a> means that white LEDs used in outdoor lighting cause substantially more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sky_glow" title="Sky glow">sky glow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDA_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-IDA-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The American Medical Association warned on the use of high blue content white LEDs in street lighting, due to their higher impact on human health and environment, compared to low blue content light sources (e.g. High-Pressure Sodium, PC amber LEDs, and low CCT LEDs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED_droop" title="LED droop">Efficiency droop</a>:</b> The efficiency of LEDs decreases as the <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a> increases. Heating also increases with higher currents which compromises the lifetime of the LED. These effects put practical limits on the current through an LED in high power applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stevenson_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-stevenson-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_LED.27s_dark_secret_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_LED.27s_dark_secret-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedaily1_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedaily1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Impact on insects:</b> LEDs are much more attractive to insects than sodium-vapor lights, so much so that there has been speculative concern about the possibility of disruption to food webs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Use in winter conditions:</b> Since they do not give off much heat in comparison to incandescent lights, LED lights used for traffic control can have snow obscuring them, leading to accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>LED uses fall into four major categories:</p>
<ul>
<li>Visual signals where light goes more or less directly from the source to the human eye, to convey a message or meaning</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">Illumination</a> where light is reflected from objects to give visual response of these objects</li>
<li>Measuring and interacting with processes involving no human vision<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></li>
<li>Narrow band light sensors where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LEDs_as_light_sensors" title="LEDs as light sensors">LEDs operate in a reverse-bias mode</a> and respond to incident light, instead of emitting light<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indicators_and_signs">Indicators and signs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Indicators and signs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">low energy consumption</a>, low maintenance and small size of LEDs has led to uses as status indicators and displays on a variety of equipment and installations. Large-area <a href="/wiki/LED_display" title="LED display">LED displays</a> are used as stadium displays, dynamic decorative displays, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynamic_message_sign" title="Dynamic message sign">dynamic message signs</a> on freeways. Thin, lightweight message displays are used at airports and railway stations, and as <a href="/wiki/Destination_sign" title="Destination sign">destination displays</a> for trains, buses, trams, and ferries.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_and_green_traffic_signals,_Stamford_Road,_Singapore_-_20111210.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3204" data-file-width="3078" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Red_and_green_traffic_signals%2C_Stamford_Road%2C_Singapore_-_20111210.jpg/170px-Red_and_green_traffic_signals%2C_Stamford_Road%2C_Singapore_-_20111210.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Red_and_green_traffic_signals%2C_Stamford_Road%2C_Singapore_-_20111210.jpg/255px-Red_and_green_traffic_signals%2C_Stamford_Road%2C_Singapore_-_20111210.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Red_and_green_traffic_signals%2C_Stamford_Road%2C_Singapore_-_20111210.jpg/340px-Red_and_green_traffic_signals%2C_Stamford_Road%2C_Singapore_-_20111210.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_and_green_traffic_signals,_Stamford_Road,_Singapore_-_20111210.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Red and green LED traffic signals</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One-color light is well suited for <a href="/wiki/Traffic_light" title="Traffic light">traffic lights</a> and signals, <a href="/wiki/Exit_sign" title="Exit sign">exit signs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emergency_vehicle_lighting" title="Emergency vehicle lighting">emergency vehicle lighting</a>, ships' navigation lights or <a href="/wiki/Lantern" title="Lantern">lanterns</a> (chromacity and luminance standards being set under the Convention on the International Regulations for Preventing Collisions at Sea 1972, Annex I and the CIE) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christmas_lighting_technology#LEDs" title="Christmas lighting technology">LED-based Christmas lights</a>. In cold climates, LED traffic lights may remain snow-covered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Red or yellow LEDs are used in indicator and alphanumeric displays in environments where night vision must be retained: aircraft cockpits, submarine and ship bridges, astronomy observatories, and in the field, e.g. night time animal watching and military field use.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LEDClose6545.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="297" data-file-width="432" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/13/LEDClose6545.jpg/220px-LEDClose6545.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/13/LEDClose6545.jpg/330px-LEDClose6545.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/13/LEDClose6545.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LEDClose6545.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Automotive applications for LEDs continue to grow.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because of their long life, fast switching times, and their ability to be seen in broad daylight due to their high output and focus, LEDs have been used in brake lights for cars' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_high-mounted_stop_lamp" title="Center high-mounted stop lamp">high-mounted brake lights</a>, trucks, and buses, and in turn signals for some time, but many vehicles now use LEDs for their rear light clusters. The use in brakes improves safety, due to a great reduction in the time needed to light fully, or faster rise time, up to 0.5 second faster<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> than an incandescent bulb. This gives drivers behind more time to react. In a dual intensity circuit (rear markers and brakes) if the LEDs are not pulsed at a fast enough frequency, they can create a <a href="/wiki/Flicker_fusion_threshold#Visual_phenomena" title="Flicker fusion threshold">phantom array</a>, where ghost images of the LED will appear if the eyes quickly scan across the array. White LED headlamps are starting to be used. Using LEDs has styling advantages because LEDs can form much thinner lights than incandescent lamps with <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">parabolic reflectors</a>.</p>
<p>Due to the relative cheapness of low output LEDs, they are also used in many temporary uses such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glowstick" title="Glowstick">glowsticks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Throwies" title="Throwies">throwies</a>, and the photonic <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textile</a> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lumalive&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lumalive (page does not exist)">Lumalive</a>. Artists have also used LEDs for <a href="/wiki/LED_art" title="LED art">LED art</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Weather_radio" title="Weather radio">Weather</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NOAA_Weather_Radio_All_Hazards" title="NOAA Weather Radio All Hazards">all-hazards</a> radio receivers with <a href="/wiki/Specific_Area_Message_Encoding" title="Specific Area Message Encoding">Specific Area Message Encoding</a> (SAME) have three LEDs: red for warnings, orange for watches, and yellow for advisories and statements whenever issued.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lighting">Lighting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Lighting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the development of high-efficiency and high-power LEDs, it has become possible to use LEDs in lighting and illumination. To encourage the shift to <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">LED lamps</a> and other high-efficiency lighting, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Energy" title="US Department of Energy">US Department of Energy</a> has created the <a href="/wiki/L_Prize" title="L Prize">L Prize</a> competition. The <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">Philips</a> Lighting North America LED bulb won the first competition on August 3, 2011, after successfully completing 18 months of intensive field, lab, and product testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>LEDs are used as <a href="/wiki/Street_light" title="Street light">street lights</a> and in other <a href="/wiki/Architectural_lighting_design" title="Architectural lighting design">architectural lighting</a>. The mechanical robustness and long lifetime are used in <a href="/wiki/Automotive_lighting" title="Automotive lighting">automotive lighting</a> on cars, motorcycles, and <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_lighting#LEDs" title="Bicycle lighting">bicycle lights</a>. LED light emission may be efficiently controlled by using <a href="/wiki/Nonimaging_optics" title="Nonimaging optics">nonimaging optics</a> principles.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/LED_street_light" title="LED street light">LED street lights</a> are employed on poles and in parking garages. In 2007, the Italian village of <a href="/wiki/Torraca" title="Torraca">Torraca</a> was the first place to convert its entire illumination system to LEDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>LEDs are used in aviation lighting. <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a> has used LED lighting in its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airbus_A320_Enhanced" title="Airbus A320 Enhanced">Airbus A320 Enhanced</a> since 2007, and Boeing uses LED lighting in the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">787</a>. LEDs are also being used now in airport and heliport lighting. LED airport fixtures currently include medium-intensity runway lights, runway centerline lights, taxiway centerline and edge lights, guidance signs, and obstruction lighting.</p>
<p>LEDs are also used as a light source for <a href="/wiki/Digital_Light_Processing" title="Digital Light Processing">DLP</a> projectors, and to <a href="/wiki/Backlight" title="Backlight">backlight</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_crystal_display" title="Liquid crystal display">LCD</a> televisions (referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED-backlit_LCD_display" title="LED-backlit LCD display">LED TVs</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Laptop" title="Laptop">laptop</a> displays. RGB LEDs raise the color gamut by as much as 45%. Screens for TV and computer displays can be made thinner using LEDs for backlighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>The lack of IR or heat radiation makes LEDs ideal for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stage_light" title="Stage light">stage lights</a> using banks of RGB LEDs that can easily change color and decrease heating from traditional stage lighting, as well as medical lighting where IR-radiation can be harmful. In energy conservation, the lower heat output of LEDs also means air conditioning (cooling) systems have less heat in need of disposal.</p>
<p>LEDs are small, durable and need little power, so they are used in handheld devices such as <a href="/wiki/Flashlight" title="Flashlight">flashlights</a>. LED <a href="/wiki/Strobe_light" title="Strobe light">strobe lights</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camera_flash" title="Camera flash">camera flashes</a> operate at a safe, low voltage, instead of the 250+ volts commonly found in <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a> flashlamp-based lighting. This is especially useful in cameras on <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile phones</a>, where space is at a premium and bulky voltage-raising circuitry is undesirable.</p>
<p>LEDs are used for infrared illumination in <a href="/wiki/Night_vision" title="Night vision">night vision</a> uses including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Security_camera" title="Security camera">security cameras</a>. A ring of LEDs around a <a href="/wiki/Video_camera" title="Video camera">video camera</a>, aimed forward into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retroreflective" title="Retroreflective">retroreflective</a> <a href="/wiki/Projection_screen" title="Projection screen">background</a>, allows <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chroma_keying" title="Chroma keying">chroma keying</a> in <a href="/wiki/Video_production" title="Video production">video productions</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LED_for_mines.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="4800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/LED_for_mines.jpg/220px-LED_for_mines.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/LED_for_mines.jpg/330px-LED_for_mines.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/LED_for_mines.jpg/440px-LED_for_mines.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LED_for_mines.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
LED to be used for miners, to increase visibility inside mines</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>LEDs are used in <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining operations</a>, as cap lamps to provide light for miners. Research has been done to improve LEDs for mining, to reduce glare and to increase illumination, reducing risk of injury to the miners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>LEDs are now used commonly in all market areas from commercial to home use: standard lighting, AV, stage, theatrical, architectural, and public installations, and wherever artificial light is used.</p>
<p>LEDs are increasingly finding uses in medical and educational applications, for example as mood enhancement,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and new technologies such as <a href="/wiki/AmBX" title="AmBX">AmBX</a>, exploiting LED versatility. <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> has even sponsored research for the use of LEDs to promote health for astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Data_communication_and_other_signalling">Data communication and other signalling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Data communication and other signalling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Li-Fi" title="Li-Fi">Li-Fi</a></div>
<p>Light can be used to transmit data and analog signals. For example, lighting white LEDs can be used in systems assisting people to navigate in closed spaces while searching necessary rooms or objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Assistive_listening_device" title="Assistive listening device">Assistive listening devices</a> in many theaters and similar spaces use arrays of infrared LEDs to send sound to listeners' receivers. Light-emitting diodes (as well as semiconductor lasers) are used to send data over many types of <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">fiber optic</a> cable, from digital audio over <a href="/wiki/TOSLINK" title="TOSLINK">TOSLINK</a> cables to the very high bandwidth fiber links that form the Internet backbone. For some time, computers were commonly equipped with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IrDA" title="IrDA">IrDA</a> interfaces, which allowed them to send and receive data to nearby machines via infrared.</p>
<p>Because LEDs can <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">cycle on and off</a> millions of times per second, very high data bandwidth can be achieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainable_lighting">Sustainable lighting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Sustainable lighting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Efficient lighting is needed for <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_architecture" title="Sustainable architecture">sustainable architecture</a>. In 2009, US Department of Energy testing results on LED lamps showed an average efficacy of 35 lm/W, below that of typical <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">CFLs</a>, and as low as 9 lm/W, worse than standard incandescent bulbs. A typical 13-watt LED lamp emitted 450 to 650 lumens,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doe2009_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-doe2009-174">[174]</a></sup> which is equivalent to a standard 40-watt incandescent bulb.</p>
<p>However, as of 2011, there are LED bulbs available as efficient as 150 lm/W and even inexpensive low-end models typically exceed 50 lm/W, so that a 6-watt LED could achieve the same results as a standard 40-watt incandescent bulb. The latter has an expected lifespan of 1,000 hours, whereas an LED can continue to operate with reduced efficiency for more than 50,000 hours.</p>
<p>See the chart below for a comparison of common light types:</p>
