<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_consumption">Energy consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Energy consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the US, one kilowatt-hour (3.6 MJ) of electricity currently causes an average 1.34 pounds (610 g) of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span> emission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Assuming the average light bulb is on for 10 hours a day, a 40-watt bulb will cause 196 pounds (89 kg) of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span> emission per year. The 6-watt LED equivalent will only cause 30 pounds (14 kg) of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span> over the same time span. A building’s carbon footprint from lighting can, therefore, be reduced by 85% by exchanging all incandescent bulbs for new LEDs if a building previously used only incandescent bulbs.</p>
<p>In practice, most buildings that use a lot of lighting use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lighting" title="Fluorescent lighting">fluorescent lighting</a>, which has 22% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luminous_efficiency" title="Luminous efficiency">luminous efficiency</a> compared with 5% for filaments, so changing to LED lighting would still give a 34% reduction in electrical power use and carbon emissions.</p>
<p>The reduction in carbon emissions depends on the source of electricity. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_the_United_States" title="Nuclear power in the United States">Nuclear power in the United States</a> produced 19.2% of electricity in 2011, so reducing electricity consumption in the U.S. reduces carbon emissions more than in France (<a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_France" title="Nuclear power in France">75% nuclear electricity</a>) or Norway (<a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Norway#Electricity_generation" title="Energy in Norway">almost entirely hydroelectric</a>).</p>
<p>Replacing lights that spend the most time lit results in the most savings, so LED lights in infrequently used locations bring a smaller return on investment.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Light_sources_for_machine_vision_systems">Light sources for machine vision systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Light sources for machine vision systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Machine_vision" title="Machine vision">Machine vision</a> systems often require bright and homogeneous illumination, so features of interest are easier to process. LEDs are often used for this purpose, and this is likely to remain one of their major uses until the price drops low enough to make signaling and illumination uses more widespread. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barcode_scanner" title="Barcode scanner">Barcode scanners</a> are the most common example of machine vision, and many low-cost products use red LEDs instead of lasers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Administrator_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Administrator-176">[176]</a></sup> Optical computer mice are an example of LEDs in machine vision, as it is used to provide an even light source on the surface for the miniature camera within the mouse. LEDs constitute a nearly ideal light source for <a href="/wiki/Machine_vision" title="Machine vision">machine vision</a> systems for several reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>The size of the illuminated field is usually comparatively small and machine vision systems are often quite expensive, so the cost of the light source is usually a minor concern. However, it might not be easy to replace a broken light source placed within complex machinery, and here the long service life of LEDs is a benefit.</li>
<li>LED elements tend to be small and can be placed with high density over flat or even-shaped substrates (PCBs etc.) so that bright and homogeneous sources that direct light from tightly controlled directions on inspected parts can be designed. This can often be obtained with small, low-cost lenses and diffusers, helping to achieve high light densities with control over lighting levels and homogeneity. LED sources can be shaped in several configurations (spot lights for reflective illumination; ring lights for coaxial illumination; backlights for contour illumination; linear assemblies; flat, large format panels; dome sources for diffused, omnidirectional illumination).</li>
<li>LEDs can be easily strobed (in the microsecond range and below) and synchronized with imaging. High-power LEDs are available allowing well-lit images even with very short light pulses. This is often used to obtain crisp and sharp "still" images of quickly moving parts.</li>
<li>LEDs come in several different colors and wavelengths, allowing easy use of the best color for each need, where different color may provide better visibility of features of interest. Having a precisely known spectrum allows tightly matched filters to be used to separate informative bandwidth or to reduce disturbing effects of ambient light. LEDs usually operate at comparatively low working temperatures, simplifying heat management, and dissipation. This allows using plastic lenses, filters, and diffusers. Waterproof units can also easily be designed, allowing use in harsh or wet environments (food, beverage, oil industries).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Administrator_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-Administrator-176">[176]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_applications">Other applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Other applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LED_Costume_by_Beo_Beyond.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2912" data-file-width="4368" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/LED_Costume_by_Beo_Beyond.jpg/220px-LED_Costume_by_Beo_Beyond.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/LED_Costume_by_Beo_Beyond.jpg/330px-LED_Costume_by_Beo_Beyond.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/LED_Costume_by_Beo_Beyond.jpg/440px-LED_Costume_by_Beo_Beyond.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LED_Costume_by_Beo_Beyond.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
LED costume for stage performers</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Digitally_printed_LED_wallpaper_Dolomites.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="2000" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Digitally_printed_LED_wallpaper_Dolomites.jpg/220px-Digitally_printed_LED_wallpaper_Dolomites.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Digitally_printed_LED_wallpaper_Dolomites.jpg/330px-Digitally_printed_LED_wallpaper_Dolomites.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Digitally_printed_LED_wallpaper_Dolomites.jpg/440px-Digitally_printed_LED_wallpaper_Dolomites.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Digitally_printed_LED_wallpaper_Dolomites.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
LED wallpaper by Meystyle</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The light from LEDs can be modulated very quickly so they are used extensively in <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">optical fiber</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_space_optics" title="Free space optics">free space optics</a> communications. This includes <a href="/wiki/Remote_control" title="Remote control">remote controls</a>, such as for TVs, VCRs, and LED Computers, where infrared LEDs are often used. <a href="/wiki/Opto-isolator" title="Opto-isolator">Opto-isolators</a> use an LED combined with a <a href="/wiki/Photodiode" title="Photodiode">photodiode</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phototransistor" title="Phototransistor">phototransistor</a> to provide a signal path with electrical isolation between two circuits. This is especially useful in medical equipment where the signals from a low-voltage <a href="/wiki/Sensor" title="Sensor">sensor</a> circuit (usually battery-powered) in contact with a living organism must be electrically isolated from any possible electrical failure in a recording or monitoring device operating at potentially dangerous voltages. An optoisolator also allows information to be transferred between circuits not sharing a common ground potential.</p>
<p>Many sensor systems rely on light as the signal source. LEDs are often ideal as a light source due to the requirements of the sensors. LEDs are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motion_sensors" title="Motion sensors">motion sensors</a>, for example in <a href="/wiki/Optical_mouse" title="Optical mouse">optical computer mice</a>. The Nintendo <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a>'s sensor bar uses infrared LEDs. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulse_oximeter" title="Pulse oximeter">Pulse oximeters</a> use them for measuring <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_saturation" title="Oxygen saturation">oxygen saturation</a>. Some flatbed scanners use arrays of RGB LEDs rather than the typical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold-cathode_fluorescent_lamp" title="Cold-cathode fluorescent lamp">cold-cathode fluorescent lamp</a> as the light source. Having independent control of three illuminated colors allows the scanner to calibrate itself for more accurate color balance, and there is no need for warm-up. Further, its sensors only need be monochromatic, since at any one time the page being scanned is only lit by one color of light. Since LEDs can also be used as photodiodes, they can be used for both photo emission and detection. This could be used, for example, in a <a href="/wiki/Touchscreen" title="Touchscreen">touchscreen</a> that registers reflected light from a finger or <a href="/wiki/Stylus" title="Stylus">stylus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Many materials and biological systems are sensitive to, or dependent on, light. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grow_lights" title="Grow lights">Grow lights</a> use LEDs to increase <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> and bacteria and viruses can be removed from water and other substances using <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV</a> LEDs for <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(microbiology)" title="Sterilization (microbiology)">sterilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-water_sterilization_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-water_sterilization-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>LEDs have also been used as a medium-quality <a href="/wiki/Voltage_reference" title="Voltage reference">voltage reference</a> in electronic circuits. The forward voltage drop (e.g. about 1.7 V for a normal red LED) can be used instead of a <a href="/wiki/Zener_diode" title="Zener diode">Zener diode</a> in low-voltage regulators. Red LEDs have the flattest I/V curve above the knee. Nitride-based LEDs have a fairly steep I/V curve and are useless for this purpose. Although LED forward voltage is far more current-dependent than a Zener diode, Zener diodes with breakdown voltages below 3 V are not widely available.</p>
<p>The progressive miniaturization of low-voltage lighting technology, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LEDs" title="LEDs">LEDs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OLEDs" title="OLEDs">OLEDs</a>, suitable to be incorporated into low-thickness materials has fostered in recent years the experimentation on combining light sources and wall covering surfaces to be applied onto interior walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The new possibilities offered by these developments have prompted some designers and companies, such as <a href="/wiki/Meystyle" title="Meystyle">Meystyle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ingo_Maurer" title="Ingo Maurer">Ingo Maurer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Lomox<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">Philips</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philips_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Philips-183">[183]</a></sup> to research and develop proprietary <a href="/wiki/LED_wallpaper" title="LED wallpaper">LED wallpaper</a> technologies, some of which are currently available for commercial purchase. Other solutions mainly exist as prototypes or are in the process of being further refined.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Light-emitting_diode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td class="navbox-list navbox-even" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
